\section{Abstract-Level Statement}

We derive the exact reduced infrared PDE of the TECT defect sector and show that, within the existing local isotropic TECT field algebra, the longitudinal Branch A channel is excluded to all local orders.
As a result, the reduced low-energy theory is forced onto an exact Branch B Dirac phase.

The reduced first-order PDE takes the form
\[
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0,
\]
which is equivalent, up to a transverse frame rotation, to an anisotropic Dirac equation with longitudinal velocity \(v_\parallel\) and transverse velocity
\[
v_\perp=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2}.
\]

The entire low-energy phenomenology is therefore controlled by the single dimensionless anisotropy ratio
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]
In the explicit concrete Class II realization, the natural regime is
\[
|\rho|\ll 1,
\]
predicting a strongly anisotropic Dirac cone, quasi-two-dimensional low-energy transport, and one-parameter universality of the infrared response.

\section{Conclusion-Level Statement}

The main structural problem is solved.

Inside the existing local isotropic TECT variable algebra, no local operator can generate a linear longitudinal tangent selector of the form
\[
\theta'(\xi)\Sigma_3.
\]
Hence the constant-leading longitudinal coefficient vanishes identically:
\[
\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}=0.
\]

Therefore the full reduced infrared theory is forced onto Branch B, with longitudinal transport arising only through the primitive odd tower
\[
a_3(\xi)
=
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}\theta'(\xi)^3
+
\frac{\lambda_c^{(2)}}{2}\theta'(\xi)^5
+\cdots.
\]

The resulting exact reduced PDE is
\[
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0,
\]
with invariant data
\[
v_\parallel,
\qquad
v_\perp=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2},
\qquad
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]

Thus the exact TECT Branch B phase predicts:
\[
\text{(i) a strongly anisotropic Dirac cone,}
\]
\[
\text{(ii) quasi-two-dimensional low-energy transport,}
\]
\[
\text{(iii) universality controlled by the single anisotropy ratio }\rho.
\]

The only remaining microscopic task is quantitative rather than structural:
to compute the coefficient tower
\[
\lambda_c^{(n)},\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)},\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}
\]
from a chosen full-action truncation and thereby determine the numerical value of \(\rho\).

\section{Ultra-Short Final Claim}

Within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra, Branch A is excluded and Branch B is exact.
The reduced defect theory is therefore an anisotropic Dirac phase,
\[
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0,
\]
whose infrared phenomenology is fully controlled by the single dimensionless ratio
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2}}.
\]
The natural explicit regime is \(|\rho|\ll 1\), implying a strongly anisotropic, quasi-two-dimensional Dirac cone.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Next theorem: nondegenerate stability of the exact Branch B phase}

\section{Statement of the next problem}

The branch problem has been reduced away: within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra,
the reduced infrared theory is on Branch B.

Therefore the next theorem is no longer about branch selection.
It is about \emph{nondegeneracy and stability} of the exact Branch B reduced PDE.

The reduced PDE is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0.
}
\label{eq:Next_PDE}
\end{equation}

The next theorem must prove
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp:=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2}\neq 0,
}
\label{eq:Next_nondeg_goal}
\end{equation}
with a quantitative robustness bound.

\section{Microscopic coefficient expansions}

We already have the exact Branch B expansions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
}
\label{eq:Next_vpar}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
}
\label{eq:Next_vtrans}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_{2n+1}
=
\int d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\theta'(\xi)^{2n+1},
\qquad
J_{2n-1}
=
\int d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\theta'(\xi)^{2n-1}\cos\theta(\xi).
}
\label{eq:Next_overlap_defs}
\end{equation}

Thus the next proof splits into two parts:

\begin{enumerate}
\item prove the relevant overlaps are nonzero;
\item prove higher-order corrections cannot cancel the leading terms without fine-tuning.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Part I: overlap nonvanishing theorem}

The first target is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3\neq 0,
\qquad
J_1\neq 0
}
\label{eq:Next_overlap_goal}
\end{equation}
for the admissible defect class.

A sufficient admissible defect class is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\theta(\xi)\) is monotone;
\item \(\theta'(\xi)\) has fixed sign and decays sufficiently fast;
\item \(f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\) is not identically zero and does not oscillate strongly enough to force cancellation.
\end{enumerate}

Under these assumptions:

\paragraph{Longitudinal overlap.}
Since \(2n+1\) is odd and \(\theta'(\xi)\) has fixed sign,
\[
\theta'(\xi)^{2n+1}
\]
has fixed sign as well.
Therefore, if \(f_+f_-\) is nonnegative on a set of nonzero measure, then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_{2n+1}\neq 0
\qquad
\text{for all }n\ge 1.
}
\label{eq:Next_Iodd_nonzero}
\end{equation}

In particular,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:Next_I3_nonzero}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Transverse overlap.}
If in addition \(\cos\theta(\xi)\) does not change sign on the defect support, or changes sign only outside the dominant support of \(f_+f_-\theta'\), then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{2n-1}\neq 0
\qquad
\text{for all relevant }n.
}
\label{eq:Next_Jodd_nonzero}
\end{equation}

In particular,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:Next_J1_nonzero}
\end{equation}

So the next proof begins by establishing a precise admissible defect class for which
\eqref{eq:Next_I3_nonzero} and \eqref{eq:Next_J1_nonzero} hold.

\section{Part II: dominance of the leading order}

Write
\begin{equation}
v_\parallel=v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_\parallel,
\qquad
v_E=v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_E,
\qquad
v_O=v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_O,
\label{eq:Next_split}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}=\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1.
}
\label{eq:Next_LO}
\end{equation}

The second target theorem is the sufficient robustness bound
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}
<
\sqrt{(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}})^2+(v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})^2}.
}
\label{eq:Next_robust_goal}
\end{equation}

Under \eqref{eq:Next_robust_goal},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:Next_robust_concl}
\end{equation}

Indeed,
\[
|v_\parallel|
\ge
|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|-|\Delta v_\parallel|>0,
\]
and similarly
\[
v_\perp
\ge
\sqrt{(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}})^2+(v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})^2}
-
\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}
>0.
\]

\section{The next theorem to prove}

Combining the two parts, the next theorem should be stated as follows.

\paragraph{Branch B nondegenerate stability theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the full theory stays within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra, so Branch B is exact;
\item the defect profile belongs to an admissible class such that
\[
I_3\neq 0,\qquad J_1\neq 0;
\]
\item the leading coefficients satisfy
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}\bigr)\neq (0,0);
\]
\item higher-order corrections obey the robustness bound \eqref{eq:Next_robust_goal}.
\end{enumerate}

Then the reduced Branch B PDE is Dirac-nondegenerate:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0,
}
\label{eq:Next_theorem_concl}
\end{equation}
and hence defines a stable anisotropic Dirac phase.

\section{Why this is the right next step}

At this point the structure theorem is already done:
the branch is fixed and the exact PDE form is known.

So the next real proof is not another reduction theorem.
It is the first theorem that converts the formal reduced PDE into a genuine stable phase statement.

Therefore the next proof target is exactly:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{prove nonvanishing of }I_3,\ J_1,\ \lambda_c^{(1)},
\text{ and }\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2},
\text{ together with a dominance bound on higher orders.}
}
\label{eq:Next_exact_target}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The next theorem is not about branch selection anymore.

It is the \emph{nondegenerate stability theorem} for the exact Branch B reduced PDE.

The exact reduced PDE is already known:
\[
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0.
\]

So the next proof must establish
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2}\neq 0.
\]

This splits into:

\[
\text{(i) overlap nonvanishing: } I_3\neq 0,\ J_1\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
\text{(ii) leading-order dominance: } |\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\quad
\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}
<
\sqrt{(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}})^2+(v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})^2}.
\]

Under these conditions, the Branch B phase is not merely formal but a genuinely stable anisotropic Dirac phase.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Overlap nonvanishing theorem for the exact Branch B phase}

\section{Goal}

We prove the first part of the Branch B nondegenerate stability theorem:
the leading longitudinal and transverse overlap integrals do not vanish for an admissible defect class.

The relevant leading overlaps are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3
=
\int_{\mathbb R} d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\theta'(\xi)^3,
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1
=
\int_{\mathbb R} d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi).
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1}
\end{equation}

\section{Admissible defect class}

We assume the following admissible defect conditions.

\paragraph{(A1) Monotone defect profile.}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta'(\xi)\ \text{has fixed sign on }\mathbb R,
\qquad
\theta'(\xi)\not\equiv 0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_A1}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(A2) Nonnegative low-mode overlap.}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
w(\xi):=f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\ge 0
\quad\text{for a.e. }\xi,
\qquad
w\not\equiv 0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_A2}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(A3) No sign flip of \(\cos\theta\) on the effective support.}
There exists a measurable set \(E\subset\mathbb R\) of positive measure such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
w(\xi)>0,\qquad
|\theta'(\xi)|>0,\qquad
\cos\theta(\xi)\ge c_0>0
\qquad
\text{for a.e. }\xi\in E.
}
\label{eq:ONV_A3}
\end{equation}

This is the natural small-amplitude defect regime.
A sufficient concrete condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\theta(\xi)|<\frac{\pi}{2}
\qquad
\text{on the support of }w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|.
}
\label{eq:ONV_small_theta}
\end{equation}

\section{Nonvanishing of \(I_3\)}

Assume first that \(\theta'(\xi)\ge 0\) for a.e. \(\xi\).
Then by \eqref{eq:ONV_A2},
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3\ge 0
\qquad\text{for a.e. }\xi.
\]
Moreover, by \eqref{eq:ONV_A1} and \eqref{eq:ONV_A2}, there exists a measurable set \(E_3\) of positive measure such that
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3>0
\qquad\text{for a.e. }\xi\in E_3.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3>0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3_pos}
\end{equation}

If instead \(\theta'(\xi)\le 0\) for a.e. \(\xi\), then
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3\le 0
\qquad\text{for a.e. }\xi,
\]
and is strictly negative on a set of positive measure.
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3<0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3_neg}
\end{equation}

Thus in either case,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3_nonzero}
\end{equation}

\section{Nonvanishing of \(J_1\)}

Again assume first that \(\theta'(\xi)\ge 0\) for a.e. \(\xi\).
Then on the set \(E\) from \eqref{eq:ONV_A3},
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)\ge c_0\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)>0.
\]
Hence
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)\ge 0
\quad\text{a.e.,}
\]
and is strictly positive on a set of positive measure.
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1>0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1_pos}
\end{equation}

If instead \(\theta'(\xi)\le 0\) for a.e. \(\xi\), then on the same set \(E\),
\[
w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)\le -c_0\,w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|<0,
\]
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1<0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1_neg}
\end{equation}

Thus in all cases,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1_nonzero}
\end{equation}

\section{Uniform lower bounds}

The previous argument gives quantitative lower bounds.

If \(\theta'(\xi)\ge 0\), then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3
\ge
\int_E d\xi\, w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3
>0,
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3_lower}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1
\ge
c_0\int_E d\xi\, w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)
>0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1_lower}
\end{equation}

If \(\theta'(\xi)\le 0\), then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|I_3|
\ge
\int_E d\xi\, w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|^3
>0,
}
\label{eq:ONV_I3_abs_lower}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|J_1|
\ge
c_0\int_E d\xi\, w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|
>0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_J1_abs_lower}
\end{equation}

\section{Corollary for the leading Branch B coefficients}

At leading order,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1.
}
\label{eq:ONV_vLO}
\end{equation}

Hence, under
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_c^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})\neq (0,0),
}
\label{eq:ONV_coupling_cond}
\end{equation}
together with \eqref{eq:ONV_I3_nonzero} and \eqref{eq:ONV_J1_nonzero}, one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}\neq 0,
\qquad
(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}},v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:ONV_vLO_nonzero}
\end{equation}

\section{Theorem}

Therefore we have proved:

\paragraph{Overlap nonvanishing theorem.}
Under the admissible defect assumptions \eqref{eq:ONV_A1}--\eqref{eq:ONV_A3},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3\neq 0,
\qquad
J_1\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:ONV_theorem}
\end{equation}

Consequently, if the leading microscopic coefficients satisfy \eqref{eq:ONV_coupling_cond}, then the leading Branch B reduced velocities are nonzero.

\section{Final Summary}

For an admissible monotone defect profile with
\[
\theta'(\xi)\ \text{of fixed sign},
\qquad
w(\xi):=f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\ge 0,\quad w\not\equiv 0,
\qquad
\cos\theta(\xi)\ge c_0>0
\]
on the effective support, the leading overlap integrals satisfy
\[
\boxed{
I_3=\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3\neq 0,
\qquad
J_1=\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)\neq 0.
}
\]

Moreover,
\[
|I_3|
\ge
\int_E d\xi\,w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|^3>0,
\qquad
|J_1|
\ge
c_0\int_E d\xi\,w(\xi)|\theta'(\xi)|>0.
\]

Therefore, if
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})\neq (0,0),
\]
then
\[
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}=\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3\neq 0,
\qquad
(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}},v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})\neq (0,0).
\]

So the first half of the Branch B nondegenerate stability theorem is proved: the leading overlaps do not vanish on the admissible defect class.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Dominance bound for the exact Branch B phase}

\section{Goal}

We now prove the second half of the Branch B nondegenerate stability theorem:
higher-order corrections do not overturn the leading nonzero velocities.

The exact Branch B coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
\qquad
v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
}
\label{eq:DB_exact_coeffs}
\end{equation}

We split them into leading and higher-order parts:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel=v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_\parallel,
\qquad
v_E=v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_E,
\qquad
v_O=v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_O,
}
\label{eq:DB_split}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}=\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1.
}
\label{eq:DB_LO}
\end{equation}

\section{Higher-order remainder}

The remainders are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
}
\label{eq:DB_dvpar}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
\Delta v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
}
\label{eq:DB_dvtrans}
\end{equation}

We seek sufficient conditions ensuring
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}
<
\sqrt{(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}})^2+(v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})^2}.
}
\label{eq:DB_goal}
\end{equation}

\section{Geometric suppression of higher overlaps}

Assume the admissible defect profile satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\theta'(\xi)|\le \Theta_*<1
\qquad\text{on the effective support of }w(\xi):=f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi),
}
\label{eq:DB_theta_bound}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\cos\theta(\xi)|\le 1.
}
\label{eq:DB_cos_bound}
\end{equation}

Then for all \(n\ge 2\),
\begin{align}
|I_{2n+1}|
&=
\left|
\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^{2n+1}
\right|
\nonumber\\
&\le
\Theta_*^{\,2n-2}
\int d\xi\,|w(\xi)|\,|\theta'(\xi)|^3
\nonumber\\
&=
\Theta_*^{\,2n-2}\,K_\parallel,
\label{eq:DB_Ibound}
\end{align}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_\parallel:=\int d\xi\,|w(\xi)|\,|\theta'(\xi)|^3.
}
\label{eq:DB_Kpar}
\end{equation}

Likewise,
\begin{align}
|J_{2n-1}|
&=
\left|
\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^{2n-1}\cos\theta(\xi)
\right|
\nonumber\\
&\le
\Theta_*^{\,2n-2}
\int d\xi\,|w(\xi)|\,|\theta'(\xi)|\,|\cos\theta(\xi)|
\nonumber\\
&\le
\Theta_*^{\,2n-2}\,K_\perp,
\label{eq:DB_Jbound}
\end{align}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_\perp:=\int d\xi\,|w(\xi)|\,|\theta'(\xi)|\,|\cos\theta(\xi)|.
}
\label{eq:DB_Kperp}
\end{equation}

Thus the higher overlaps are geometrically suppressed by powers of \(\Theta_*^2\).

\section{Microscopic coefficient bounds}

Assume there exist constants \(L_*\), \(E_*\), \(O_*\) such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\lambda_c^{(n)}|\le L_*\,R_\parallel^{\,n-1}\,|\lambda_c^{(1)}|,
\qquad n\ge 2,
}
\label{eq:DB_lam_bound}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}|
\le
E_*\,R_\perp^{\,n-1}\,|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|,
\qquad
|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}|
\le
O_*\,R_\perp^{\,n-1}\,|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|,
\qquad n\ge 2,
}
\label{eq:DB_trans_bound}
\end{equation}
for some nonnegative growth factors \(R_\parallel,R_\perp\).

This is the natural finite-growth assumption: higher-order microscopic coefficients do not grow faster than geometrically.

\section{Longitudinal dominance bound}

Using \eqref{eq:DB_dvpar}, \eqref{eq:DB_Ibound}, and \eqref{eq:DB_lam_bound},
\begin{align}
|\Delta v_\parallel|
&\le
\sum_{n\ge 2}\frac{|\lambda_c^{(n)}|}{2}|I_{2n+1}|
\nonumber\\
&\le
\frac{L_*|\lambda_c^{(1)}|K_\parallel}{2}
\sum_{n\ge 2}(R_\parallel\Theta_*^2)^{n-1}.
\label{eq:DB_dvpar_bound1}
\end{align}

If
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_\parallel\Theta_*^2<1,
}
\label{eq:DB_geom_cond_par}
\end{equation}
then the series converges and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|
\le
\frac{L_*|\lambda_c^{(1)}|K_\parallel}{2}\,
\frac{R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}.
}
\label{eq:DB_dvpar_bound2}
\end{equation}

Now, from the overlap nonvanishing theorem we have
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|I_3|\ge m_\parallel>0
}
\label{eq:DB_I3_lower}
\end{equation}
for some defect-dependent lower bound \(m_\parallel\).
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}|I_3|
\ge
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}m_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:DB_vparLO_lower}
\end{equation}

Therefore a sufficient condition for longitudinal dominance is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*K_\parallel\,
\frac{R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}
<
m_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:DB_long_suff}
\end{equation}

Under \eqref{eq:DB_long_suff},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|.
}
\label{eq:DB_long_done}
\end{equation}

\section{Transverse dominance bound}

Define the leading transverse norm
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}
:=
\sqrt{(v_E^{\mathrm{LO}})^2+(v_O^{\mathrm{LO}})^2}
=
|J_1|\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}.
}
\label{eq:DB_vperpLO}
\end{equation}

Also let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\perp
:=
\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}.
}
\label{eq:DB_dvperp}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:DB_dvtrans}, \eqref{eq:DB_Jbound}, and \eqref{eq:DB_trans_bound},
\begin{align}
|\Delta v_E|
&\le
E_*|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|K_\perp
\sum_{n\ge 2}(R_\perp\Theta_*^2)^{n-1},
\label{eq:DB_dvE_bound}
\\
|\Delta v_O|
&\le
O_*|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|K_\perp
\sum_{n\ge 2}(R_\perp\Theta_*^2)^{n-1}.
\label{eq:DB_dvO_bound}
\end{align}

If
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_\perp\Theta_*^2<1,
}
\label{eq:DB_geom_cond_perp}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_E|
\le
E_*|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|K_\perp
\frac{R_\perp\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\perp\Theta_*^2},
}
\label{eq:DB_dvE_bound2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_O|
\le
O_*|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|K_\perp
\frac{R_\perp\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\perp\Theta_*^2}.
}
\label{eq:DB_dvO_bound2}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\perp
\le
K_\perp\frac{R_\perp\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\perp\Theta_*^2}
\sqrt{
E_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2
+
O_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}.
}
\label{eq:DB_dvperp_bound}
\end{equation}

From the overlap nonvanishing theorem,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|J_1|\ge m_\perp>0.
}
\label{eq:DB_J1_lower}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}
\ge
m_\perp\sqrt{
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}.
}
\label{eq:DB_vperpLO_lower}
\end{equation}

A sufficient condition for transverse dominance is thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_\perp\frac{R_\perp\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\perp\Theta_*^2}
\sqrt{
E_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2
+
O_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}
<
m_\perp\sqrt{
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}.
}
\label{eq:DB_trans_suff}
\end{equation}

Under \eqref{eq:DB_trans_suff},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\perp<v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}.
}
\label{eq:DB_trans_done}
\end{equation}

\section{Nondegenerate stability theorem}

Combining \eqref{eq:DB_long_done} and \eqref{eq:DB_trans_done}, we obtain:

\paragraph{Branch B nondegenerate stability theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the theory remains within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra, so Branch B is exact;
\item the defect belongs to the admissible class, so
\[
I_3\neq 0,\qquad J_1\neq 0;
\]
\item the higher overlap suppression condition \eqref{eq:DB_theta_bound} holds with \(\Theta_*<1\);
\item the microscopic coefficient towers satisfy the finite-growth bounds \eqref{eq:DB_lam_bound} and \eqref{eq:DB_trans_bound};
\item the sufficient inequalities \eqref{eq:DB_long_suff} and \eqref{eq:DB_trans_suff} hold.
\end{enumerate}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:DB_theorem_concl}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact reduced Branch B PDE defines a stable anisotropic Dirac phase.

\section{Interpretation}

The theorem shows that once the defect is monotone and the microscopic higher-order tower is not too violently enhanced,
the leading primitive Branch B transport dominates all higher odd corrections.

Thus the exact Dirac phase is robust, not merely formal.
The remaining quantitative task is only to estimate the numerical sizes of
\[
\Theta_*,\qquad
R_\parallel,\qquad
R_\perp,\qquad
L_*,\qquad
E_*,\qquad
O_*.
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The higher-order corrections are
\[
\Delta v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
\qquad
\Delta v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
\Delta v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
\]

If the defect is monotone with
\[
|\theta'(\xi)|\le \Theta_*<1,
\]
then the higher overlaps are geometrically suppressed:
\[
|I_{2n+1}|\lesssim \Theta_*^{2n-2}K_\parallel,
\qquad
|J_{2n-1}|\lesssim \Theta_*^{2n-2}K_\perp.
\]

If the microscopic coefficient towers grow at most geometrically,
\[
|\lambda_c^{(n)}|\le L_*R_\parallel^{n-1}|\lambda_c^{(1)}|,
\]
\[
|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}|\le E_*R_\perp^{n-1}|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|,
\qquad
|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}|\le O_*R_\perp^{n-1}|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|,
\]
and if
\[
L_*K_\parallel\frac{R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\parallel\Theta_*^2}<m_\parallel,
\]
\[
K_\perp\frac{R_\perp\Theta_*^2}{1-R_\perp\Theta_*^2}
\sqrt{
E_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+O_*^2(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}
<
m_\perp
\sqrt{
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
},
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0.
}
\]

So the exact Branch B reduced PDE is a genuinely stable anisotropic Dirac phase, provided the higher-order tower is sufficiently suppressed relative to the leading primitive term.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Next theorem: generic validity of the Branch B stability bounds}

\paragraph{Goal.}
Show that the sufficient dominance bounds for the exact Branch B phase hold on an open natural parameter regime of the microscopic TECT defect data.

\paragraph{Data.}
The exact Branch B reduced coefficients are
\[
v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
\qquad
v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
\]

The leading-order concrete Class II terms are
\[
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,g_c\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,g_E\,\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,g_O\,\theta_0\kappa.
\]

\paragraph{Natural small-defect regime.}
Assume
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
and that the higher microscopic coefficient towers
\[
\lambda_c^{(n)},\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)},\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}
\]
have at most finite geometric growth.

Then the higher-order overlaps are suppressed by powers of \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\), so
\[
|\Delta v_\parallel|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^5),
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\]
while
\[
|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\qquad
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}=O(\theta_0\kappa).
\]

Therefore the relative errors obey
\[
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}
=
O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
\qquad
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}
=
O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
\]

Hence, for sufficiently small but nonzero \(\theta_0\kappa\),
\[
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp<v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}.
\]

So the exact Branch B phase is generically nondegenerate on an open natural small-defect parameter regime.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Generic stability of the exact Branch B phase in the natural small-defect regime}

\section{Goal}

We prove that the sufficient dominance bounds for the exact Branch B phase hold generically on an open small-defect regime.

The exact reduced coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
\qquad
v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_exact}
\end{equation}

We want to show that, in the natural small-defect regime,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp<v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}},
}
\label{eq:NSD_goal}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\Delta v_\perp:=\sqrt{(\Delta v_E)^2+(\Delta v_O)^2}.
\]

\section{Leading-order structure}

Write
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel=v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_\parallel,
\qquad
v_E=v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_E,
\qquad
v_O=v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}+\Delta v_O.
}
\label{eq:NSD_split}
\end{equation}

At leading order,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}=\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}=\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1.
}
\label{eq:NSD_LO}
\end{equation}

The remainders are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvpar}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
\Delta v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 2}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvtrans}
\end{equation}

\section{Natural small-defect scaling}

Assume the defect profile has the standard small-amplitude, finite-width scaling
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta(\xi)=\theta_0\,\Theta(\kappa\xi),
}
\label{eq:NSD_theta_profile}
\end{equation}
where \(\Theta\) is a fixed dimensionless profile with bounded derivatives and \(\theta_0,\kappa>0\).

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta'(\xi)=\theta_0\kappa\,\Theta'(\kappa\xi).
}
\label{eq:NSD_theta_prime}
\end{equation}

Hence each odd overlap scales as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_{2n+1}
=
(\theta_0\kappa)^{2n+1}\,\mathcal I_{2n+1},
}
\label{eq:NSD_I_scaling}
\end{equation}
with dimensionless profile-dependent constants
\[
\mathcal I_{2n+1}
=
\int d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\Theta'(\kappa\xi)^{2n+1}.
\]

Likewise,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_{2n-1}
=
(\theta_0\kappa)^{2n-1}\,\mathcal J_{2n-1},
}
\label{eq:NSD_J_scaling}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\mathcal J_{2n-1}
=
\int d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,\Theta'(\kappa\xi)^{2n-1}\cos(\theta_0\Theta(\kappa\xi)).
\]

For sufficiently small \(\theta_0\), one has
\[
\cos(\theta_0\Theta)=1+O(\theta_0^2),
\]
so the leading scaling of \(J_{2n-1}\) is unchanged.

Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\qquad
I_5=O((\theta_0\kappa)^5),
\qquad
I_7=O((\theta_0\kappa)^7),\dots
}
\label{eq:NSD_I_orders}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_1=O(\theta_0\kappa),
\qquad
J_3=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\qquad
J_5=O((\theta_0\kappa)^5),\dots
}
\label{eq:NSD_J_orders}
\end{equation}

\section{Finite-growth assumption on the microscopic towers}

Assume the coefficient towers do not grow singularly as \(\theta_0\kappa\to 0\).
More precisely, assume there exist finite constants
\[
C_\parallel,\qquad C_E,\qquad C_O
\]
such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sup_{n\ge 1}\left|\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{\lambda_c^{(1)}}\right|\le C_\parallel,
\qquad
\sup_{n\ge 1}\left|\frac{\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}}{\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}}\right|\le C_E,
\qquad
\sup_{n\ge 1}\left|\frac{\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}}{\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}}\right|\le C_O.
}
\label{eq:NSD_finite_growth}
\end{equation}

This is the natural regularity assumption: higher-order microscopic coefficients remain of finite relative size and do not blow up fast enough to overcome the small-defect suppression.

\section{Longitudinal remainder estimate}

Using \eqref{eq:NSD_dvpar}, \eqref{eq:NSD_I_scaling}, and \eqref{eq:NSD_finite_growth},
\begin{align}
|\Delta v_\parallel|
&\le
\sum_{n\ge 2}\frac{|\lambda_c^{(n)}|}{2}|I_{2n+1}|
\nonumber\\
&\le
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}C_\parallel
\sum_{n\ge 2}(\theta_0\kappa)^{2n+1}|\mathcal I_{2n+1}|.
\label{eq:NSD_dvpar_bound1}
\end{align}

Assume also that the profile constants obey
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\mathcal I_{2n+1}|\le M_\parallel
}
\label{eq:NSD_I_uniform}
\end{equation}
uniformly in the range of interest. Then
\begin{align}
|\Delta v_\parallel|
&\le
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}C_\parallel M_\parallel
\sum_{n\ge 2}(\theta_0\kappa)^{2n+1}
\nonumber\\
&=
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}C_\parallel M_\parallel
(\theta_0\kappa)^5
\sum_{m\ge 0}(\theta_0\kappa)^{2m}.
\label{eq:NSD_dvpar_bound2}
\end{align}

If \((\theta_0\kappa)^2<1\), this gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|
\le
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}C_\parallel M_\parallel
\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^5}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvpar_bound3}
\end{equation}

Meanwhile
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}|I_3|
=
\frac{|\lambda_c^{(1)}|}{2}(\theta_0\kappa)^3|\mathcal I_3|.
}
\label{eq:NSD_vpar_LO_bound}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}
\le
\frac{C_\parallel M_\parallel}{|\mathcal I_3|}
\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_long_ratio}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
}
\label{eq:NSD_long_bigO}
\end{equation}

\section{Transverse remainder estimate}

Similarly,
\begin{align}
|\Delta v_E|
&\le
|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|C_E
\sum_{n\ge 2}|J_{2n-1}|,
\label{eq:NSD_dvE_bound1}
\\
|\Delta v_O|
&\le
|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|C_O
\sum_{n\ge 2}|J_{2n-1}|.
\label{eq:NSD_dvO_bound1}
\end{align}

Assume
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\mathcal J_{2n-1}|\le M_\perp
}
\label{eq:NSD_J_uniform}
\end{equation}
uniformly. Then by \eqref{eq:NSD_J_scaling},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|J_{2n-1}|
\le
M_\perp(\theta_0\kappa)^{2n-1}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_J_bound}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_E|
\le
|\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}|C_EM_\perp
\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^3}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2},
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvE_bound2}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_O|
\le
|\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}|C_OM_\perp
\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^3}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvO_bound2}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta v_\perp
\le
M_\perp\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^3}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2}
\sqrt{
C_E^2(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2
+
C_O^2(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_dvperp_bound}
\end{equation}

On the other hand,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
|J_1|\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}
=
(\theta_0\kappa)|\mathcal J_1|
\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_vperp_LO_bound}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}
\le
\frac{M_\perp}{|\mathcal J_1|}
\frac{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}{1-(\theta_0\kappa)^2}
\frac{
\sqrt{
C_E^2(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2
+
C_O^2(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}
}{
\sqrt{
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2
}
}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_trans_ratio}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
}
\label{eq:NSD_trans_bigO}
\end{equation}

\section{Generic stability theorem}

Combining \eqref{eq:NSD_long_bigO} and \eqref{eq:NSD_trans_bigO}, we obtain:

\paragraph{Generic small-defect stability theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact theory stays on Branch B;
\item the defect belongs to the admissible class so that \(\mathcal I_3\neq 0\) and \(\mathcal J_1\neq 0\);
\item the profile constants \(\mathcal I_{2n+1},\mathcal J_{2n-1}\) are uniformly bounded in the small-defect regime;
\item the microscopic towers \(\lambda_c^{(n)},\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}\) satisfy the finite-growth bounds \eqref{eq:NSD_finite_growth}.
\end{enumerate}

Then, as \(\theta_0\kappa\to 0\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
\qquad
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
}
\label{eq:NSD_generic_ratios}
\end{equation}

Therefore there exists \(\varepsilon_*>0\) such that whenever
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa<\varepsilon_*,
\]
one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp<v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}.
}
\label{eq:NSD_generic_dom}
\end{equation}

Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:NSD_generic_nondeg}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact Branch B reduced PDE defines a generically stable anisotropic Dirac phase on an open natural small-defect regime.

\section{Interpretation}

This theorem means that the sufficient stability inequalities are not fine-tuned.
They are automatically satisfied in an open region of parameter space as long as the defect is sufficiently weak and the microscopic coefficient towers remain regular.

So the Branch B phase is not merely allowed; it is the natural generic outcome of the existing isotropic local TECT algebra in the small-defect regime.

\section{Final Summary}

In the natural small-defect regime
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
the odd overlap tower scales as
\[
I_{2n+1}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^{2n+1}),
\qquad
J_{2n-1}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^{2n-1}).
\]

If the microscopic coefficient towers have finite relative growth, then
\[
|\Delta v_\parallel|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^5),
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\]
while
\[
|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^3),
\qquad
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}=O(\theta_0\kappa).
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
\qquad
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
}
\]

Hence, for sufficiently small but nonzero \(\theta_0\kappa\),
\[
|\Delta v_\parallel|<|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|,
\qquad
\Delta v_\perp<v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}.
\]

So the exact Branch B reduced PDE is generically nondegenerate and stable on an open natural small-defect regime.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Exact Branch B Phase Theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Exact Branch B phase theorem]
Assume the following.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The full reduced defect theory stays within the existing local isotropic TECT variable algebra, so that no linear longitudinal selector is generated and hence
\[
\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}=0.
\]

\item The defect belongs to the admissible monotone class:
\[
\theta'(\xi)\ \text{has fixed sign},\qquad
w(\xi):=f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\ge 0,\quad w\not\equiv 0,
\]
and
\[
\cos\theta(\xi)\ge c_0>0
\]
on the effective support.

\item The leading microscopic coefficients satisfy
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
\bigl(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}\bigr)\neq (0,0).
\]

\item The defect lies in the natural small-defect regime
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
and the higher microscopic coefficient towers have finite relative growth.
\end{enumerate}

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the longitudinal sector is exactly Branch B:
\[
a_3(\xi)
=
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}\theta'(\xi)^3
+
\frac{\lambda_c^{(2)}}{2}\theta'(\xi)^5
+\cdots;
\]

\item the leading overlaps satisfy
\[
I_3\neq 0,
\qquad
J_1\neq 0;
\]

\item the higher-order corrections are subleading:
\[
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
\qquad
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2);
\]

\item therefore
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp:=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2}\neq 0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Hence the reduced infrared defect PDE is an exact stable anisotropic Dirac phase of Branch B type:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0.
}
\label{eq:Exact_BranchB_Phase}
\end{equation}

Moreover, up to transverse frame rotation and overall rescaling, the phase is characterized by the single dimensionless anisotropy ratio
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Branch A exclusion follows from the absence of a linear longitudinal selector inside the existing local isotropic TECT algebra.
The exact concrete Class II longitudinal/transverse split is
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\frac{\lambda_c}{2}I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
\alpha=I_\theta\Lambda_E,
\qquad
\beta=I_\theta\Lambda_O,
\]
so the concrete branch is primitive in the longitudinal sector and \(I_\theta\)-type in the transverse sector. :contentReference[oaicite:0]{index=0} :contentReference[oaicite:1]{index=1}

The overlap nonvanishing gives
\[
I_{\theta'^3}\neq 0,
\qquad
I_\theta\neq 0,
\]
for nontrivial admissible defects, so the finite nondegeneracy condition reduces to
\[
\lambda_c\neq 0,
\qquad
(\Lambda_E,\Lambda_O)\neq (0,0). \text{ :contentReference[oaicite:2]{index=2}}
\]

The exact concrete Class II witnesses are explicitly
\[
\lambda_\parallel=\frac{g_c}{2}I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
\alpha=g_E I_\theta,
\qquad
\beta=g_O I_\theta,
\]
with
\[
I_{\theta'^3}
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\qquad
I_\theta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\]
so the leading hierarchy is explicit. :contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3} :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

Finally, in the small-defect regime \(0<\theta_0\kappa\ll1\), the odd longitudinal tower and odd transverse tower are suppressed by successive powers of \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\), so the higher-order remainders cannot overturn the nonzero leading terms under finite-growth assumptions on the microscopic coefficient towers. Therefore \(v_\parallel\neq 0\) and \(v_\perp\neq 0\), proving stable Dirac nondegeneracy.
\end{proof}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Next frontier: microscopic truncation prediction for the anisotropy ratio}

\paragraph{Goal.}
The structural phase theorem is already closed.
The next frontier is quantitative:
predict the anisotropy ratio
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}
\]
from a concrete microscopic truncation of the full TECT action.

\paragraph{Leading-order prediction.}
At leading order in the explicit concrete Branch B realization,
\[
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3,
\qquad
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1,
\qquad
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1,
\]
so
\[
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
|J_1|\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}.
\]

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3}{
|J_1|\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}
}.
}
\label{eq:rho_truncation_LO}
\end{equation}

Using the explicit overlap values of the concrete Class II realization,
\[
I_3=-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\qquad
J_1=-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\]
this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:rho_truncation_explicit}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{What must be computed microscopically.}
Thus the only remaining microscopic inputs are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_c^{(1)},
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}.
}
\label{eq:rho_needed_inputs}
\end{equation}

These are obtained from the tangent first-order Hessian block
\[
V^\mu(\xi)=\Pi_Q\mathbb H_1^\mu(\xi)\Pi_Q
\]
through
\[
a_3(\xi)=\frac12\Tr_{Q_B}(\Sigma_3\,n_\mu V^\mu),
\qquad
b_E(\xi)=\frac12\Tr_{Q_B}(\Sigma_a\,E_\mu{}^aV^\mu),
\qquad
b_O(\xi)=\frac12\Tr_{Q_B}(\Sigma_a\,J_\mu{}^aV^\mu),
\]
followed by the leading projections
\[
a_3(\xi)=\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}\theta'(\xi)^3+\cdots,
\qquad
b_E(\xi)=\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)+\cdots,
\qquad
b_O(\xi)=\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)+\cdots.
\]

\paragraph{Natural expectation.}
If
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)},\ \widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\ \widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}
\]
are all of the same microscopic order, then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim (\theta_0\kappa)^2\ll 1
}
\label{eq:rho_natural_again}
\end{equation}
in the natural small-defect regime.

\paragraph{Fine-tuned balanced regime.}
To obtain
\[
|\rho|\sim 1,
\]
one needs
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\lambda_c^{(1)}|
\sim
(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\sqrt{(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)})^2+(\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})^2},
}
\label{eq:rho_balanced_finetune}
\end{equation}
which requires strong enhancement of the primitive longitudinal coefficient.

\paragraph{Immediate next task.}
So the immediate next quantitative task is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{choose a concrete full-action truncation, compute }
\lambda_c^{(1)},\ \widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\ \widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)},
\text{ and evaluate }\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}.
}
\label{eq:rho_next_task}
\end{equation}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Concrete one-mediator truncation and explicit prediction for \texorpdfstring{$\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}$}{rho_LO}}

\section{Minimal predictive truncation}

Take the following concrete lowest-order truncation:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{trunc}}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{core}}
+
\mathcal L_{P}^{\mathrm{geom}}
+
\mathcal L_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{vol}}
+
\frac12 M_X^2 X_i^a X_i^a
+
\alpha_X X_i^a J_i^a[\Psi]
+
\beta_X X_i^a K_i^a[P,\psi]
+
\gamma_E X_i^a \mathcal E_i^a[P,\psi]
+
\gamma_O X_i^a \mathcal O_i^a[P,\psi].
}
\label{eq:CT_trunc}
\end{equation}

Here:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_i^a[\Psi]
=
(\partial_i\Psi^\dagger)T_a\Psi+\Psi^\dagger T_a(\partial_i\Psi),
}
\label{eq:CT_J}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_i^a[P,\psi]
=
\Tr(T_a\partial_iP)\,n^\mu D_\mu\psi,
}
\label{eq:CT_K}
\end{equation}

and the explicit transverse carriers are chosen as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal E_i^a[P,\psi]
=
\Tr(T_a\partial_iP)\,\Tr(S_E M_E)\,E^\mu D_\mu\psi,
}
\label{eq:CT_E}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_i^a[P,\psi]
=
\Tr(T_a\partial_iP)\,\Tr(S_O M_O)\,J^\mu D_\mu\psi.
}
\label{eq:CT_O}
\end{equation}

This is the simplest one-mediator truncation that carries:
\[
\text{(i) primitive longitudinal source via }J\cdot K,
\qquad
\text{(ii) transverse even/odd sources via }K\cdot \mathcal E,\ K\cdot \mathcal O.
\]

\section{Integrating out the mediator}

The algebraic equation of motion for \(X_i^a\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_X^2 X_i^a
+
\alpha_X J_i^a
+
\beta_X K_i^a
+
\gamma_E \mathcal E_i^a
+
\gamma_O \mathcal O_i^a
=
0.
}
\label{eq:CT_Xeom}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X_i^a
=
-\frac{1}{M_X^2}
\bigl(
\alpha_X J_i^a+\beta_X K_i^a+\gamma_E\mathcal E_i^a+\gamma_O\mathcal O_i^a
\bigr).
}
\label{eq:CT_Xsol}
\end{equation}

Substituting back gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
-\frac{1}{2M_X^2}
\bigl(
\alpha_X J_i^a+\beta_X K_i^a+\gamma_E\mathcal E_i^a+\gamma_O\mathcal O_i^a
\bigr)^2.
}
\label{eq:CT_Leff}
\end{equation}

The relevant cross terms are:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
\supset
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}J_i^aK_i^a
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}K_i^a\mathcal E_i^a
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}K_i^a\mathcal O_i^a.
}
\label{eq:CT_relevant_cross}
\end{equation}

\section{Longitudinal coefficient}

On the explicit real defect representative,
the primitive longitudinal contraction is controlled by
\[
A_a(\theta)k_a(\theta)=1
\]
in the previously fixed normalization.
More generally, let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\parallel:=A_a(\theta)k_a(\theta)\big|_{\mathrm{normalized}},
}
\label{eq:CT_Cpar}
\end{equation}
so that in the explicit concrete normalization
\[
C_\parallel=1.
\]

Then the primitive longitudinal transport coefficient is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_c^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}\,C_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:CT_lambda_c}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{\lambda_c^{(1)}}{2}I_3
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{2M_X^2}C_\parallel\,I_3.
}
\label{eq:CT_vpar}
\end{equation}

\section{Transverse coefficients}

Likewise define the normalized transverse overlap constants
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_E:=\text{normalized coefficient of }K_i^a\mathcal E_i^a,
\qquad
C_O:=\text{normalized coefficient of }K_i^a\mathcal O_i^a.
}
\label{eq:CT_CEO}
\end{equation}

For the explicit representative with
\[
\Tr(S_E M_E)=\cos\theta,
\qquad
\Tr(S_O M_O)=\cos\theta,
\]
and the canonical basis choice, one may take
\[
C_E=C_O=1.
\]

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}\,C_E,
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}\,C_O.
}
\label{eq:CT_LambdaEO}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_E^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}J_1
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}C_E\,J_1,
}
\label{eq:CT_vE}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_O^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}J_1
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}C_O\,J_1.
}
\label{eq:CT_vO}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{|\beta_X|}{M_X^2}|J_1|
\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:CT_vperp}
\end{equation}

\section{Explicit prediction for \texorpdfstring{$\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}$}{rho_LO}}

Now
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X C_\parallel}{2\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}
\frac{I_3}{|J_1|}.
}
\label{eq:CT_rho_general}
\end{equation}

Using the explicit concrete overlap values
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_3
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
\qquad
J_1
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:CT_I3J1}
\end{equation}
we get
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{I_3}{|J_1|}
=
-\frac{3}{16}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:CT_ratio_overlaps}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:CT_rho_explicit}
\end{equation}

In the canonical explicit normalization
\[
C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1,
\]
this simplifies to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:CT_rho_canonical}
\end{equation}

\section{Interpretation}

This truncation yields three immediate conclusions.

\paragraph{(i) Mediator scale cancellation.}
The common factor \(\beta_X/M_X^2\) cancels out of the ratio \(\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}\).
Thus \(\rho\) is controlled not by the overall mediator strength, but by the relative coupling hierarchy
\[
\alpha_X : \gamma_E : \gamma_O.
\]

\paragraph{(ii) Natural anisotropy.}
If
\[
\alpha_X,\ \gamma_E,\ \gamma_O
\]
are all of the same microscopic order, then
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim (\theta_0\kappa)^2\ll 1.
\]

\paragraph{(iii) Balanced cone requires enhancement.}
To obtain \(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim 1\), one needs
\[
|\alpha_X|
\sim
(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2},
\]
which is a strong longitudinal enhancement.

\section{Minimal predictive theorem}

Therefore, within the one-mediator truncation \eqref{eq:CT_trunc}, the leading anisotropy ratio is completely predicted by the microscopic coupling ratio:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:CT_predictive_theorem}
\end{equation}

This is the first fully explicit microscopic prediction of the Branch B phase.

\section{Final Summary}

For the simplest one-mediator full-action truncation,
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{trunc}}
=
\cdots
+\frac12 M_X^2 X_i^aX_i^a
+\alpha_X X_i^aJ_i^a
+\beta_X X_i^aK_i^a
+\gamma_E X_i^a\mathcal E_i^a
+\gamma_O X_i^a\mathcal O_i^a,
\]
integrating out \(X_i^a\) gives
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}C_\parallel,
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}C_E,
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}C_O.
\]

Hence
\[
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

In the canonical explicit normalization \(C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1\),
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

So the leading anisotropy is controlled only by the relative microscopic coupling hierarchy
\[
\alpha_X : \gamma_E : \gamma_O,
\]
while the common mediator strength cancels out.

Therefore the natural expectation remains
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll 1
\]
unless the longitudinal coupling \(\alpha_X\) is strongly enhanced.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Naturalness theorem for the coupling hierarchy}

\section{Starting point}

For the concrete one-mediator truncation, the leading anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Nat_rho_start}
\end{equation}

In the canonical explicit normalization
\[
C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1,
\]
this reduces to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Nat_rho_canonical}
\end{equation}

Thus the only non-geometric factor controlling the size of \(\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}\) is the coupling ratio
\[
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}.
\]

\section{Symmetry hypothesis}

Assume the mediator \(X_i^a\) couples to a unified shell-current multiplet
\[
\mathbb J_i^a
=
c_\parallel\,J_i^a
+
c_E\,\mathcal E_i^a
+
c_O\,\mathcal O_i^a,
\]
through a single bare coupling \(g_X\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g_X\,X_i^a\,\mathbb J_i^a.
}
\label{eq:Nat_unified_int}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, the three couplings are not fundamentally independent but arise as projections of one common microscopic interaction:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O.
}
\label{eq:Nat_projected_couplings}
\end{equation}

Here \(c_\parallel,c_E,c_O\) are dimensionless representation/projection coefficients.

\section{O(1) projection hypothesis}

Assume further that the shell-current projections are symmetry-allowed and non-fine-tuned, namely
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_\parallel,\ c_E,\ c_O = O(1),
\qquad
(c_E,c_O)\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:Nat_O1}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}
=
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
=
O(1).
}
\label{eq:Nat_ratio_O1}
\end{equation}

Therefore, from \eqref{eq:Nat_rho_canonical},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
}
\label{eq:Nat_rho_scaling}
\end{equation}

Hence in the natural small-defect regime
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
one obtains
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll 1.
}
\label{eq:Nat_rho_small}
\end{equation}

\section{Naturalness theorem}

We may therefore state:

\paragraph{Coupling naturalness theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the longitudinal and transverse mediator couplings arise from a single unified microscopic mediator interaction;
\item the corresponding projection coefficients \(c_\parallel,c_E,c_O\) are symmetry-allowed and \(O(1)\);
\item the defect lies in the natural small-defect regime \(0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1\).
\end{enumerate}

Then the leading anisotropy ratio satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2)\ll 1.
}
\label{eq:Nat_theorem}
\end{equation}

Thus the strongly anisotropic Branch B cone is the natural generic prediction.

\section{When can balanced cones occur?}

From \eqref{eq:Nat_rho_canonical}, a balanced cone \(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim 1\) requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\alpha_X|
\sim
(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:Nat_balanced}
\end{equation}

Under the unified-coupling hypothesis \eqref{eq:Nat_projected_couplings}, this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|c_\parallel|
\sim
(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:Nat_balanced_proj}
\end{equation}

Since \((\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}\gg 1\) in the small-defect regime, this requires either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a strong symmetry-breaking enhancement of the longitudinal projection;
\item an accidental cancellation in the transverse projections;
\item or a breakdown of the \(O(1)\) projection hypothesis.
\end{enumerate}

Thus \(|\rho|\sim 1\) is fine-tuned relative to the natural Branch B expectation.

\section{Honest scope}

This theorem does \emph{not} prove that the full microscopic TECT action must realize the unified-coupling structure \eqref{eq:Nat_projected_couplings}.
What it proves is the following:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{If the mediator couplings come from one symmetry multiplet with }O(1)\text{ projections, then }|\rho|\ll 1\text{ is natural.}
}
\label{eq:Nat_scope}
\end{equation}

So the remaining microscopic task is to identify whether the actual full-action truncation realizes this unified-coupling structure.

\section{Final Summary}

The leading anisotropy ratio is
\[
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

If the three couplings arise from one unified mediator interaction,
\[
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O,
\]
with
\[
c_\parallel,\ c_E,\ c_O = O(1),
\]
then
\[
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}
=
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
=
O(1).
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2)\ll 1
}
\]
in the natural small-defect regime.

So the strongly anisotropic Branch B cone does not require tuning.
It is the natural generic prediction whenever the longitudinal and transverse mediator couplings come from one common symmetry multiplet with \(O(1)\) projection coefficients.

By contrast, a balanced cone \(|\rho|\sim 1\) requires a large enhancement of the longitudinal projection or an accidental suppression of the transverse ones, and is therefore fine-tuned.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Exact distribution of the anisotropy prefactor under isotropic unified coupling}

\section{Starting point}

Under the unified-coupling hypothesis,
\[
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O,
\]
the leading anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Dist_rhoLO}
\end{equation}

Define the dimensionless coupling ratio
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r:=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
}
\label{eq:Dist_r}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Dist_rho_abs}
\end{equation}

\section{Isotropic multiplet hypothesis}

Assume the projection vector
\[
(c_\parallel,c_E,c_O)
\]
is distributed isotropically on the unit sphere
\[
c_\parallel^2+c_E^2+c_O^2=1.
\]

Then we may parametrize
\[
c_\parallel=\cos\vartheta,
\qquad
c_E=\sin\vartheta\cos\varphi,
\qquad
c_O=\sin\vartheta\sin\varphi,
\]
with the standard rotationally invariant measure
\[
d\mu=\frac{1}{4\pi}\sin\vartheta\,d\vartheta\,d\varphi.
\]

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r=\frac{|\cos\vartheta|}{\sin\vartheta}=|\cot\vartheta|.
}
\label{eq:Dist_r_cot}
\end{equation}

\section{Distribution of \(r\)}

For an isotropic unit vector in three dimensions, the variable
\[
z:=c_\parallel=\cos\vartheta
\]
is uniformly distributed on \([-1,1]\).
Therefore \(|z|\) is uniformly distributed on \([0,1]\).

Since
\[
r=\frac{|z|}{\sqrt{1-z^2}},
\]
the cumulative distribution function of \(r\) is
\begin{align}
F_r(R)
&=
\mathbb P(r\le R)
\nonumber\\
&=
\mathbb P\!\left(
\frac{|z|}{\sqrt{1-z^2}}\le R
\right)
\nonumber\\
&=
\mathbb P\!\left(
|z|^2\le \frac{R^2}{1+R^2}
\right)
\nonumber\\
&=
\mathbb P\!\left(
|z|\le \frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}}
\right).
\label{eq:Dist_F_intermediate}
\end{align}

Since \(|z|\sim \mathrm{Unif}[0,1]\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}},
\qquad
R\ge 0.
}
\label{eq:Dist_CDF}
\end{equation}

Differentiating gives the density:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
f_r(R)=\frac{1}{(1+R^2)^{3/2}},
\qquad
R\ge 0.
}
\label{eq:Dist_density}
\end{equation}

\section{Typical values}

\subsection{Median}

The median \(r_{\mathrm{med}}\) satisfies
\[
F_r(r_{\mathrm{med}})=\frac12.
\]
Thus
\[
\frac{r_{\mathrm{med}}}{\sqrt{1+r_{\mathrm{med}}^2}}=\frac12.
\]
Squaring gives
\[
4r_{\mathrm{med}}^2=1+r_{\mathrm{med}}^2,
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3}\approx 0.577.
}
\label{eq:Dist_median}
\end{equation}

Therefore the median anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0541\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Dist_rho_median}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Probability of a balanced cone}

A balanced cone would require \(r\gtrsim (\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}\).
Using \eqref{eq:Dist_CDF},
\begin{align}
\mathbb P(r\ge R)
&=
1-\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}}.
\label{eq:Dist_tail}
\end{align}

For large \(R\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb P(r\ge R)
=
\frac{1}{2R^2}+O(R^{-4}).
}
\label{eq:Dist_tail_asymp}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb P\!\left(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim O(1)\right)
\sim
O\!\left((\theta_0\kappa)^4\right)
}
\label{eq:Dist_balanced_prob}
\end{equation}
in the natural small-defect regime.

So balanced cones are strongly statistically suppressed.

\subsection{Probability that \(r<1\)}

As a simple benchmark,
\[
F_r(1)=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\approx 0.707.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb P(r<1)=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\approx 70.7\%.
}
\label{eq:Dist_r_lt1}
\end{equation}

So even before multiplying by the small geometric factor \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\), the coupling ratio is typically not large.

\section{Expectation values}

The mean of \(r\) diverges:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb E[r]=\infty.
}
\label{eq:Dist_mean_div}
\end{equation}
Indeed,
\[
\int_0^\infty R\,f_r(R)\,dR
=
\int_0^\infty \frac{R}{(1+R^2)^{3/2}}\,dR
\]
has a logarithmic divergence at infinity.

Thus the mean is not a good notion of “typical size”.
The median and tail bounds are the correct robust descriptors.

\section{Microscopic consequence for TECT}

Combining \eqref{eq:Dist_rho_abs} with \eqref{eq:Dist_median} gives the typical estimate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{typ}}
\sim
O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
}
\label{eq:Dist_typical}
\end{equation}
with a concrete median value
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.0541\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

Therefore, under isotropic unified coupling, the strongly anisotropic Branch B cone is not merely allowed:
it is the statistically typical outcome.

\section{Final theorem}

\paragraph{Isotropic unified-coupling theorem.}
Assume the mediator coupling projections \((c_\parallel,c_E,c_O)\) form an isotropically distributed unit vector in the unified coupling space.
Then the prefactor
\[
r=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
\]
has exact distribution
\[
f_r(R)=\frac{1}{(1+R^2)^{3/2}},
\qquad
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}}.
\]

Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2
}
\label{eq:Dist_finalrho}
\end{equation}
is typically of order \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\), with median
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:Dist_finalmedian}
\end{equation}

Thus the generic Branch B prediction is a strongly anisotropic cone, while balanced cones are statistically suppressed.

\section{Final Summary}

If the unified coupling projections \((c_\parallel,c_E,c_O)\) are isotropic on the unit sphere, then
\[
r:=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
\]
has exact CDF and density
\[
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}},
\qquad
f_r(R)=\frac{1}{(1+R^2)^{3/2}}.
\]

Therefore
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\]
has typical size
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{typ}}\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
\]

More concretely, the median is
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0541\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Also,
\[
\mathbb P(r<1)=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\approx 70.7\%,
\]
and the probability of a balanced cone is parametrically suppressed:
\[
\mathbb P(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\sim O(1))\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^4).
\]

So under isotropic unified coupling, the strongly anisotropic Branch B cone is the statistically typical TECT prediction.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Numerical examples for the anisotropy ratio}

Using the isotropic unified-coupling distribution, the leading anisotropy ratio is
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}r(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

The median corresponds to
\[
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3},
\]
so
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx 0.0541(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

The 90th percentile corresponds to
\[
r_{0.9}=\frac{0.9}{\sqrt{1-0.9^2}}\approx 2.0647,
\]
so
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{90\%}
\approx 0.1936(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 5.41\times 10^{-4},
\qquad
|\rho|_{90\%}\approx 1.94\times 10^{-3}.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.2
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 2.16\times 10^{-3},
\qquad
|\rho|_{90\%}\approx 7.74\times 10^{-3}.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.3
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 4.87\times 10^{-3},
\qquad
|\rho|_{90\%}\approx 1.74\times 10^{-2}.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.5
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 1.35\times 10^{-2},
\qquad
|\rho|_{90\%}\approx 4.84\times 10^{-2}.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=1.0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 5.41\times 10^{-2},
\qquad
|\rho|_{90\%}\approx 1.94\times 10^{-1}.
\]

\section{Exact probability formula for \texorpdfstring{$|\rho|<\rho_*$}{|rho|<rho*}}

Let
\[
a:=\frac{3}{32},
\qquad
x:=\theta_0\kappa.
\]
Then
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=a\,r\,x^2.
\]

Hence
\[
\mathbb P(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|<\rho_*)
=
\mathbb P\!\left(
r<\frac{\rho_*}{a x^2}
\right)
=
F_r\!\left(\frac{\rho_*}{a x^2}\right).
\]

Using
\[
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}},
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb P(|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|<\rho_*)
=
\frac{u}{\sqrt{1+u^2}},
\qquad
u:=\frac{32\,\rho_*}{3(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:prob_rho_exact}
\end{equation}

\section{Numerical examples}

For \(\rho_*=0.1\):

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.1)\approx 0.999956.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.2
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.1)\approx 0.999297.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.3
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.1)\approx 0.996490.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.5
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.1)\approx 0.973646.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=1.0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.1)\approx 0.729537.
\]

For \(\rho_*=0.01\):

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.01)\approx 0.995327.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.2
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.01)\approx 0.936329.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.3
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.01)\approx 0.764600.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.5
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.01)\approx 0.392480.
\]

\[
\theta_0\kappa=1.0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathbb P(|\rho|<0.01)\approx 0.106070.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Group-velocity anisotropy and cone aspect ratio}

\section{Definitions}

For the exact Branch B reduced PDE,
\[
\omega(\mathbf p)^2
=
v_\parallel^2 p_\parallel^2
+
v_\perp^2(p_1^2+p_2^2),
\qquad
\rho:=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]

Define the cone aspect ratio
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A:=\frac{p_\parallel^{\max}}{p_\perp^{\max}}=\frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|}=\frac{1}{|\rho|}.
}
\label{eq:GV_Adef}
\end{equation}

The characteristic group-velocity anisotropy is likewise
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|v_{g,\perp}|}{|v_{g,\parallel}|}\sim \frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|}=\frac{1}{|\rho|}=A.
}
\label{eq:GV_Vratio}
\end{equation}

Thus the cone aspect ratio and the velocity anisotropy ratio are the same invariant quantity.

\section{Median estimate under isotropic unified coupling}

Under isotropic unified coupling,
\[
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3},
\]
hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx 0.0541266\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:GV_rhomed}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{1}{|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\approx 18.475\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
}
\label{eq:GV_Amed}
\end{equation}

\section{Numerical table}

The median numerical predictions are:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c|c}
\theta_0\kappa
&
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}
&
A_{\mathrm{med}}=\dfrac{1}{|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}}
&
\dfrac{|v_{g,\perp}|}{|v_{g,\parallel}|}\sim A_{\mathrm{med}}
\\
\hline
0.1 & 5.41\times 10^{-4} & 1.85\times 10^{3} & 1.85\times 10^{3}
\\
0.2 & 2.17\times 10^{-3} & 4.62\times 10^{2} & 4.62\times 10^{2}
\\
0.3 & 4.87\times 10^{-3} & 2.05\times 10^{2} & 2.05\times 10^{2}
\\
0.5 & 1.35\times 10^{-2} & 7.39\times 10^{1} & 7.39\times 10^{1}
\\
1.0 & 5.41\times 10^{-2} & 1.85\times 10^{1} & 1.85\times 10^{1}
\end{array}
\]

\section{90th-percentile table}

Using
\[
r_{0.9}\approx 2.0647,
\]
we get
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho|_{90\%}
\approx 0.1936\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
A_{90\%}\approx 5.165\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
}
\label{eq:GV_90}
\end{equation}

Hence:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\theta_0\kappa
&
|\rho|_{90\%}
&
A_{90\%}=\dfrac{1}{|\rho|_{90\%}}
\\
\hline
0.1 & 1.94\times 10^{-3} & 5.16\times 10^{2}
\\
0.2 & 7.74\times 10^{-3} & 1.29\times 10^{2}
\\
0.3 & 1.74\times 10^{-2} & 5.74\times 10^{1}
\\
0.5 & 4.84\times 10^{-2} & 2.07\times 10^{1}
\\
1.0 & 1.94\times 10^{-1} & 5.16
\end{array}
\]

\section{Interpretation}

These numbers imply:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Even at \(\theta_0\kappa=1\), the median cone remains strongly anisotropic:
\[
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 18.5.
\]

\item In the genuinely small-defect regime \(\theta_0\kappa\le 0.3\), the typical anisotropy is enormous:
\[
A_{\mathrm{med}}\gtrsim 2\times 10^2.
\]

\item Therefore the generic Branch B prediction is not merely anisotropy, but an extremely transverse-dominated Dirac cone.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Compressed consequence}

Thus the Branch B phase predicts
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}),
\qquad
\frac{|v_{g,\perp}|}{|v_{g,\parallel}|}\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}),
}
\label{eq:GV_compressed}
\end{equation}
with a large numerical prefactor already at the median level.

\section{Final Summary}

Under isotropic unified coupling, the median anisotropy ratio is
\[
|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx 0.0541266\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

Therefore the cone aspect ratio and the group-velocity anisotropy are both
\[
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{1}{|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\approx 18.475\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
\]

Numerically:

\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.1
\Rightarrow
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 1.85\times 10^3,
\]
\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.2
\Rightarrow
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 4.62\times 10^2,
\]
\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.3
\Rightarrow
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 2.05\times 10^2,
\]
\[
\theta_0\kappa=0.5
\Rightarrow
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 7.39\times 10^1,
\]
\[
\theta_0\kappa=1.0
\Rightarrow
A_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 1.85\times 10^1.
\]

Thus the generic Branch B prediction is an extremely transverse-dominated Dirac cone:
\[
\boxed{
A\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}),
\qquad
\frac{|v_{g,\perp}|}{|v_{g,\parallel}|}\sim O((\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}).
}
\]
