\section{Inverse estimation of the defect parameter from the cone aspect ratio}

\section{Starting point}

At leading order, the anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
r:=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_rho}
\end{equation}

The cone aspect ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A:=\frac{1}{|\rho|}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_Adef}
\end{equation}

Therefore, at leading order,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{32}{3r}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_A_LO}
\end{equation}

\section{Direct inverse formula}

Solving \eqref{eq:Inv_A_LO} for the defect parameter gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_theta_general}
\end{equation}

Thus a measurement of the aspect ratio \(A\) determines the combination \(\theta_0\kappa\) up to the coupling-projection factor \(r\).

\section{Velocity-ratio form}

Since
\[
A=\frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|},
\]
we also have
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{
\frac{32}{3r}\,
\frac{|v_\parallel|}{v_\perp}
}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_theta_vel}
\end{equation}

Hence the same inverse estimate may be extracted either from the cone geometry or from the group-velocity anisotropy.

\section{Median inverse estimate under isotropic unified coupling}

Under isotropic unified coupling, the median value is
\[
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3}.
\]
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\sqrt{\frac{32\sqrt3}{3A}}
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_theta_median}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\approx
18.475\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_A_median}
\end{equation}

\section{Numerical inverse examples}

Using the median inverse formula \eqref{eq:Inv_theta_median}:

\[
A=10
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
1.36.
\]

\[
A=20
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.961.
\]

\[
A=50
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.608.
\]

\[
A=100
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.430.
\]

\[
A=200
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.304.
\]

\[
A=500
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.192.
\]

\[
A=1000
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
0.136.
\]

\section{Credible interval from coupling uncertainty}

If the coupling factor \(r\) is not known exactly but lies in an interval
\[
r\in[r_{\min},r_{\max}],
\qquad
0<r_{\min}\le r_{\max},
\]
then the inferred defect parameter lies in the interval
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r_{\max}A}}
\le
\theta_0\kappa
\le
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r_{\min}A}}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_interval}
\end{equation}

Thus the uncertainty in \(\theta_0\kappa\) is only square-root sensitive to the uncertainty in \(r\).

\section{Tail-based inference}

From the exact isotropic unified-coupling distribution
\[
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}},
\]
we may derive confidence-style inversion rules.

Given a confidence level \(p\in(0,1)\), define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_p=\frac{p}{\sqrt{1-p^2}}.
}
\label{eq:Inv_rp}
\end{equation}

Then
\[
\mathbb P(r\le r_p)=p,
\]
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb P\!\left(
\theta_0\kappa
\ge
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r_pA}}
\right)=p.
}
\label{eq:Inv_prob_bound}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, one may use \(r_p\) to construct percentile-based estimates of \(\theta_0\kappa\).

\section{Compressed practical formula}

For practical purposes, the cleanest inversion rule is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}
\qquad
\text{(median estimate under isotropic unified coupling).}
}
\label{eq:Inv_practical}
\end{equation}

Thus measuring the cone aspect ratio immediately gives an estimate of the effective defect strength parameter.

\section{Interpretation}

This inversion formula has a clear meaning:

\begin{enumerate}
\item very large aspect ratio \(A\) implies a weak/small defect parameter \(\theta_0\kappa\);
\item moderate aspect ratio \(A\sim 10\!-\!20\) corresponds to \(\theta_0\kappa\sim O(1)\);
\item the small-defect regime \(\theta_0\kappa\ll 1\) corresponds to very large aspect ratios \(A\gg 10\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the observed cone anisotropy provides a direct low-energy probe of the microscopic defect geometry.

\section{Final Summary}

The leading relation between the cone aspect ratio and the defect parameter is
\[
A=\frac{1}{|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|}
=
\frac{32}{3r}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2},
\qquad
r=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
\]

Hence the inverse estimate is
\[
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}}.
}
\]

Under isotropic unified coupling, the median value \(r_{\mathrm{med}}=1/\sqrt3\) gives
\[
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\sqrt{\frac{32\sqrt3}{3A}}
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}.
}
\]

Examples:
\[
A=100 \Rightarrow (\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 0.430,
\qquad
A=200 \Rightarrow (\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 0.304,
\qquad
A=1000 \Rightarrow (\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx 0.136.
\]

Thus measuring the cone aspect ratio directly probes the microscopic defect-strength combination \(\theta_0\kappa\).
Large aspect ratio means small defect strength; small-defect regimes correspond to very large anisotropy.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Error propagation from the cone aspect ratio to the defect parameter}

\section{Starting point}

The leading inverse relation is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}},
}
\label{eq:Err_theta_general}
\end{equation}
where
\[
A=\frac{1}{|\rho|},
\qquad
r=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
\]

For the median isotropic unified-coupling estimate,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}.
}
\label{eq:Err_theta_median}
\end{equation}

\section{Differential error propagation at fixed \(r\)}

Treat \(r\) as fixed first.
Let
\[
x:=\theta_0\kappa.
\]
From \eqref{eq:Err_theta_general},
\[
x=C\,A^{-1/2},
\qquad
C:=\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r}}.
\]

Differentiate:
\[
dx
=
-\frac12 C\,A^{-3/2}\,dA.
\]
Since \(x=CA^{-1/2}\), this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{dx}{x}
=
-\frac12\,\frac{dA}{A}.
}
\label{eq:Err_logdiff}
\end{equation}

Hence, for small observational uncertainty,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
\frac12\,\frac{\delta A}{A}.
}
\label{eq:Err_relative}
\end{equation}

Therefore the inferred defect parameter is square-root stable with respect to aspect-ratio uncertainty.

\section{Absolute error formula}

From \eqref{eq:Err_theta_general},
\[
\frac{\partial(\theta_0\kappa)}{\partial A}
=
-\frac12\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r}}\,A^{-3/2}.
\]
Hence the absolute propagated error is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)
\approx
\frac12\sqrt{\frac{32}{3r}}\,A^{-3/2}\,\delta A.
}
\label{eq:Err_absolute_general}
\end{equation}

Using \(\theta_0\kappa=\sqrt{32/(3rA)}\), this may also be written as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)
\approx
\frac{\theta_0\kappa}{2A}\,\delta A.
}
\label{eq:Err_absolute_compact}
\end{equation}

For the median isotropic estimate,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)
\approx
\frac{4.298}{2}\,A^{-3/2}\,\delta A
=
2.149\,A^{-3/2}\,\delta A.
}
\label{eq:Err_absolute_median}
\end{equation}

\section{Examples}

\paragraph{Example 1: \(A=100\).}
Then
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx \frac{4.298}{10}=0.430.
\]

If \(\delta A=10\) (a \(10\%\) relative uncertainty), then
\[
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
\frac12\frac{10}{100}=0.05,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)\approx 0.0215.
}
\]

Thus
\[
\theta_0\kappa\approx 0.430\pm 0.022.
\]

\paragraph{Example 2: \(A=200\).}
Then
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx \frac{4.298}{\sqrt{200}}\approx 0.304.
\]

If \(\delta A=20\) (again \(10\%\)), then
\[
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}=0.05,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)\approx 0.0152.
}
\]

Thus
\[
\theta_0\kappa\approx 0.304\pm 0.015.
\]

\paragraph{Example 3: \(A=1000\).}
Then
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}\approx \frac{4.298}{\sqrt{1000}}\approx 0.136.
\]

If \(\delta A=100\) (again \(10\%\)), then
\[
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}=0.05,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)\approx 0.0068.
}
\]

Thus
\[
\theta_0\kappa\approx 0.136\pm 0.0068.
\]

\section{Including uncertainty in \(r\)}

If the coupling-projection factor \(r\) is also uncertain, then
\[
\theta_0\kappa
=
\left(\frac{32}{3}\right)^{1/2} r^{-1/2}A^{-1/2}.
\]
Taking logarithmic differentials gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
-\frac12\frac{dA}{A}
-\frac12\frac{dr}{r}.
}
\label{eq:Err_with_r_log}
\end{equation}

Hence, for independent small uncertainties,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\left(\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}\right)^2
\approx
\frac14\left(\frac{\delta A}{A}\right)^2
+
\frac14\left(\frac{\delta r}{r}\right)^2.
}
\label{eq:Err_with_r_quad}
\end{equation}

So the relative uncertainty in \(r\) enters on the same square-root footing as the relative uncertainty in \(A\).

\section{Confidence interval form}

If one uses the median estimator for \(r\), then the dominant observational uncertainty comes from \(A\), and the practical propagated interval is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}
\left(
1\pm \frac12\frac{\delta A}{A}
\right)
}
\label{eq:Err_practical_interval}
\end{equation}
to leading order in \(\delta A/A\).

\section{Interpretation}

The error propagation has a favorable structure:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the inferred defect parameter depends only on \(A^{-1/2}\), not \(A^{-1}\);
\item therefore relative errors are reduced by a factor of \(2\);
\item large aspect-ratio measurements still allow reasonably stable estimation of \(\theta_0\kappa\).
\end{enumerate}

Thus cone anisotropy is not only a theoretically meaningful observable but also a quantitatively robust probe of the defect-strength scale.

\section{Final Summary}

From the inverse relation
\[
\theta_0\kappa=\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}},
\]
the leading small-error propagation at fixed \(r\) is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
\frac12\,\frac{\delta A}{A}.
}
\]

So the defect parameter is square-root stable with respect to aspect-ratio uncertainty.

Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)
\approx
\frac{\theta_0\kappa}{2A}\,\delta A.
}
\]

Under the median isotropic unified-coupling estimate,
\[
\theta_0\kappa\approx \frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}},
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)\approx 2.149\,A^{-3/2}\,\delta A.
}
\]

Examples for a \(10\%\) relative uncertainty in \(A\):

\[
A=100 \Rightarrow \theta_0\kappa\approx 0.430\pm 0.022,
\]
\[
A=200 \Rightarrow \theta_0\kappa\approx 0.304\pm 0.015,
\]
\[
A=1000 \Rightarrow \theta_0\kappa\approx 0.136\pm 0.0068.
\]

If \(r\) is also uncertain, then
\[
\boxed{
\left(\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}\right)^2
\approx
\frac14\left(\frac{\delta A}{A}\right)^2
+
\frac14\left(\frac{\delta r}{r}\right)^2.
}
\]

Thus aspect-ratio measurements provide a quantitatively robust probe of the microscopic defect-strength combination \(\theta_0\kappa\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final anisotropy--defect theorem and observational corollary}

\begin{theorem}[Anisotropy--defect theorem for the exact Branch B phase]
Assume the exact Branch B phase theorem holds, so that the reduced infrared defect PDE is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0,
}
\label{eq:ADT_PDE}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp:=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2},
\qquad
\rho:=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_rho}
\end{equation}

Then the cone aspect ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A:=\frac{p_\parallel^{\max}}{p_\perp^{\max}}=\frac{1}{|\rho|}=\frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_A}
\end{equation}

For the concrete one-mediator Branch B truncation with unified coupling projections
\[
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O,
\]
define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r:=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_r}
\end{equation}

Then the leading anisotropy ratio satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\label{eq:ADT_rhoLO}
\end{equation}
equivalently
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{32}{3r}\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_ALO}
\end{equation}

Therefore the effective defect-strength parameter is determined by the observable anisotropy through
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_inverse}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact Branch B phase has the following interpretation:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the low-energy anisotropy is not merely qualitative but quantitatively encodes the microscopic defect-strength combination }\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:ADT_main_message}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}[Median observational estimate under isotropic unified coupling]
Assume further that the projection vector
\[
(c_\parallel,c_E,c_O)
\]
is isotropically distributed on the unit sphere in coupling space.
Then the median value of the coupling factor is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_rmed}
\end{equation}

Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0541266\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\label{eq:ADT_rhomed}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\approx
18.475\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2},
}
\label{eq:ADT_Amed}
\end{equation}
and the median inverse estimate is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\sqrt{\frac{32\sqrt3}{3A}}
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_theta_med}
\end{equation}
\end{corollary}

\begin{corollary}[Error-propagation corollary]
At fixed \(r\), the leading inverse relation
\[
\theta_0\kappa=\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}}
\]
implies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
\frac12\,\frac{\delta A}{A}.
}
\label{eq:ADT_error_rel}
\end{equation}

Thus the inferred defect parameter is square-root stable with respect to aspect-ratio uncertainty.
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta(\theta_0\kappa)
\approx
\frac{\theta_0\kappa}{2A}\,\delta A.
}
\label{eq:ADT_error_abs}
\end{equation}
\end{corollary}

\begin{corollary}[Natural-regime corollary]
If the small-defect regime holds,
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
and the unified coupling projections are \(O(1)\), then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll 1,
\qquad
A_{\mathrm{LO}}\gg 1.
}
\label{eq:ADT_small_defect}
\end{equation}

Therefore the generic explicit Branch B prediction is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a strongly transverse-dominated anisotropic Dirac cone whose measured aspect ratio directly probes the underlying defect strength.}
}
\label{eq:ADT_physical}
\end{equation}
\end{corollary}

\section{Final Summary}

For the exact Branch B phase,
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp},
\qquad
A=\frac{1}{|\rho|}=\frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|}.
\]

In the concrete one-mediator unified-coupling truncation,
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,r\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
r=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}},
\]
so
\[
A_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{32}{3r}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2},
\qquad
\theta_0\kappa
=
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}}.
\]

Thus the observable anisotropy and the microscopic defect strength are directly linked.

Under isotropic unified coupling,
\[
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3},
\]
hence
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2},
\]
and
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)_{\mathrm{med}}
\approx
\frac{4.298}{\sqrt{A}}.
\]

Moreover, at fixed \(r\),
\[
\frac{\delta(\theta_0\kappa)}{\theta_0\kappa}
=
\frac12\,\frac{\delta A}{A}.
\]

Therefore the exact Branch B phase predicts that large cone anisotropy is the natural signature of weak defects, and the measured aspect ratio provides a quantitatively robust probe of the microscopic defect-strength combination \(\theta_0\kappa\).

\section{PROJECT: TECT --- Master Contents for Continuation (Updated Snapshot)}

\section{Project Goal}

The long-term goal of TECT (Topological Energy Condensate Theory) is to derive, from a microscopic condensate/defect framework:

\begin{enumerate}
\item emergent spacetime-like defect kinematics,
\item emergent protected fermionic infrared sectors,
\item eventually gauge structure and realistic matter phenomenology.
\end{enumerate}

At the present stage, the most rigorously developed part is the emergent defect-fermion Dirac sector.

\section{Working Rules}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Conversation language: Korean.
\item Mathematical derivations: English LaTeX.
\item Always provide:
\begin{enumerate}
\item short Korean explanation,
\item full detailed LaTeX derivation,
\item final summary (LaTeX),
\item next step.
\end{enumerate}
\item Avoid philosophical exposition.
\item Focus on rigorous mathematical derivations and honest separation between what is proved, conditionally proved, and open.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Global Status}

The old scalar Bragg-pair route is no longer the correct mainline.
Within the currently known safe microscopic operator library, the scalar identity-channel Bragg block remains nonzero and cannot be canceled by the known safe scalar/alignment/Class II sectors.
Therefore the correct mainline is the graded \(2+2\) reduced route.

The reduced Dirac theorem is already closed.
The actual local \(CP^2\) projector-defect geometry is compatible with the S2 branch.
The branch ambiguity has now been pushed much further and, within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra, Branch B is effectively exact.

\section{What is Fully Closed}

\subsection{Reduced graded Dirac theorem}

The reduced first-order symbol must take the S2 form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_1(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma_3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2).
}
\label{eq:MC_A1}
\end{equation}

The exact nondegeneracy condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq (0,0).
}
\label{eq:MC_nondeg}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Actual local defect geometry}

For the local projector-defect background
\[
P_B(\xi)=z_B(\xi)z_B(\xi)^\dagger,
\qquad
z_B^\dagger z_B=1,
\]
the tangent bundle is
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb Cz_B(\xi))^\perp.
\]

Using the explicit real representative
\[
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\sin\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
e_1(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
-\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\cos\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
e_2=
\begin{pmatrix}
0\\
1\\
0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
the tangent Pauli operators are
\[
\Sigma_1,\Sigma_2,\Sigma_3.
\]

\subsection{Scalar-channel no-go}

Inside the existing safe local isotropic operator library, the scalar identity-channel Bragg block cannot be removed.
So the old scalar mainline is ruled out.

\subsection{Natural S2 branch and transverse finite test}

The reduced symbol is
\[
A_1(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma_3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2),
\]
and the transverse sector is reduced to the finite nonzero test
\[
(\Lambda_E,\Lambda_O)\neq (0,0).
\]

\section{Branch A / Branch B Status}

\subsection{Original branch question}

The old unresolved longitudinal question was:
\[
\text{Branch A: }\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}\neq 0,
\qquad
\text{Branch B: }\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}=0,\ \lambda_c\neq 0.
\]

\subsection{Minimal complete LO realization}

For the minimal complete lowest-order TECT realization already explicitly constructed,
\[
\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}=0,
\qquad
\lambda_c\neq 0.
\]
Therefore the explicit concrete currently constructed branch is Branch B.

\subsection{Why Branch A fails in the existing local isotropic algebra}

For the real defect representative,
\[
\Pi_Q \mathcal T_\xi \Pi_Q=0,
\qquad
(\Pi_Q\mathcal T_\xi^2\Pi_Q)_{\mathrm{tr}}
=
\frac12\theta'(\xi)^2\Sigma_3.
\]

Therefore no linear longitudinal tangent selector of the form
\[
\theta'(\xi)\Sigma_3
\]
can be generated from the existing local isotropic TECT algebra.

This remains true not only at lowest order but to all local orders within the same isotropic local variable algebra.
Higher local corrections generate only the odd primitive tower:
\[
\theta'^3\Sigma_3,\qquad
\theta'^5\Sigma_3,\qquad
\theta'^7\Sigma_3,\ \dots
\]

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda_\parallel^{(0)}=0
}
\end{equation}
is exact inside the existing local isotropic TECT algebra.

\subsection{Exact Branch B conclusion}

Hence, within the existing local isotropic TECT framework, Branch B is exact.

\section{Exact Branch B Reduced PDE}

The exact reduced Branch B PDE is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Bigl[
i\partial_t
-
v_\parallel\,\sigma_3(-i\partial_\parallel)
-
v_E\bigl(\sigma_1(-i\partial_1)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_2)\bigr)
-
v_O\bigl(-\sigma_1(-i\partial_2)+\sigma_2(-i\partial_1)\bigr)
\Bigr]\chi=0,
}
\label{eq:MC_exact_BranchB}
\end{equation}
with
\[
v_\perp:=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2},
\qquad
\rho:=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]

Up to transverse frame rotation and overall rescaling, the infrared phenomenology is controlled by the single anisotropy ratio \(\rho\).

\section{Exact Branch B Phase Theorem}

The current main theorem proved/assembled in conditional-exact form is:

\paragraph{Exact Branch B phase theorem.}
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the theory stays within the existing local isotropic TECT variable algebra,
\item the defect belongs to an admissible monotone class,
\item the leading microscopic coefficients satisfy
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}\neq 0,
\qquad
(\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)})\neq (0,0),
\]
\item the defect lies in the natural small-defect regime
\[
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
and higher coefficient towers have finite relative growth.
\end{enumerate}

Then:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Branch B is exact,
\item \(I_3\neq 0\) and \(J_1\neq 0\),
\item higher-order corrections are subleading,
\item hence
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\perp\neq 0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the reduced defect PDE defines a stable anisotropic Dirac phase.

\section{Overlap Nonvanishing Theorem}

For admissible monotone defects with
\[
\theta'(\xi)\ \text{of fixed sign},
\qquad
w(\xi):=f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\ge 0,\quad w\not\equiv 0,
\qquad
\cos\theta(\xi)\ge c_0>0
\]
on the effective support, one has
\[
I_3=\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)^3\neq 0,
\qquad
J_1=\int d\xi\,w(\xi)\theta'(\xi)\cos\theta(\xi)\neq 0.
\]

\section{Higher-Order Dominance}

The exact coefficients are
\[
v_\parallel
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\frac{\lambda_c^{(n)}}{2}I_{2n+1},
\qquad
v_E
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(n)}J_{2n-1},
\qquad
v_O
=
\sum_{n\ge 1}\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(n)}J_{2n-1}.
\]

In the natural small-defect regime
\[
\theta(\xi)=\theta_0\Theta(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
0<\theta_0\kappa\ll 1,
\]
the overlap towers scale as
\[
I_{2n+1}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^{2n+1}),
\qquad
J_{2n-1}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^{2n-1}),
\]
so the higher-order remainders satisfy
\[
\frac{|\Delta v_\parallel|}{|v_\parallel^{\mathrm{LO}}|}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2),
\qquad
\frac{\Delta v_\perp}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{LO}}}=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2).
\]

Thus the Branch B phase is generically stable in an open natural small-defect regime.

\section{Concrete One-Mediator Truncation}

A concrete predictive truncation was introduced:
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{trunc}}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{core}}
+
\mathcal L_{P}^{\mathrm{geom}}
+
\mathcal L_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{vol}}
+
\frac12 M_X^2 X_i^a X_i^a
+
\alpha_X X_i^a J_i^a[\Psi]
+
\beta_X X_i^a K_i^a[P,\psi]
+
\gamma_E X_i^a \mathcal E_i^a[P,\psi]
+
\gamma_O X_i^a \mathcal O_i^a[P,\psi].
\]

Integrating out \(X_i^a\) gives
\[
\lambda_c^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}C_\parallel,
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}C_E,
\qquad
\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}
=
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}C_O.
\]

In canonical explicit normalization,
\[
C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1.
\]

\section{Leading Anisotropy Ratio}

In the one-mediator truncation,
\[
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]

In canonical normalization,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:MC_rho_LO}
\end{equation}

Important consequence:
\[
\beta_X,\ M_X
\]
drop out of the ratio \(\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}\).
So the anisotropy is controlled by the relative coupling hierarchy
\[
\alpha_X:\gamma_E:\gamma_O
\]
and the geometric factor \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\).

\section{Unified-Coupling Naturalness}

If the couplings arise from a single mediator multiplet:
\[
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O,
\]
with
\[
c_\parallel,\ c_E,\ c_O = O(1),
\]
then
\[
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}
=
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
=
O(1).
\]

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2)\ll 1
}
\end{equation}
in the natural small-defect regime.
So the strongly anisotropic Branch B cone is the natural generic prediction.

Balanced cones \(|\rho|\sim 1\) require strong enhancement of the longitudinal projection or accidental suppression of the transverse ones, and are therefore fine-tuned.

\section{Isotropic Unified-Coupling Distribution}

Under the isotropic unified-coupling hypothesis, the projection vector
\[
(c_\parallel,c_E,c_O)
\]
is isotropically distributed on the unit sphere.
Define
\[
r:=\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}.
\]

Then
\[
F_r(R)=\frac{R}{\sqrt{1+R^2}},
\qquad
f_r(R)=\frac{1}{(1+R^2)^{3/2}}.
\]

The median is
\[
r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3}.
\]

Hence
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt3}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx 0.0541266\,(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\]
and
\[
A_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac{1}{|\rho|_{\mathrm{med}}}
=
\frac{32\sqrt3}{3}(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}
\approx 18.475\,(\theta_0\kappa)^{-2}.
\]

Balanced cones are statistically suppressed.

\section{Numerical Anisotropy Examples}

\]

\]

Thus the natural Branch B prediction is not merely mild anisotropy but an extremely transverse-dominated Dirac cone.

\section{Anisotropy--Defect Theorem}

For the exact Branch B phase,
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp},
\qquad
A=\frac{1}{|\rho|}=\frac{v_\perp}{|v_\parallel|}.
\]

In the concrete unified-coupling truncation,
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|

\theta_0\kappa

\label{eq:MC_inverse}
\end{equation}

Thus the observable low-energy anisotropy directly encodes the microscopic defect-strength combination \(\theta_0\kappa\).

\section{Median Inverse Estimate}

Under isotropic unified coupling,

r_{\mathrm{med}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt3},

so the practical median inversion rule is

\theta_0\kappa
\approx

\section{Error Propagation}

At fixed \(r\),

\theta_0\kappa=\sqrt{\frac{32}{3rA}},

so

Hence a \(10\%\) relative uncertainty in \(A\) leads to only a \(5\%\) relative uncertainty in the inferred \(\theta_0\kappa\).

Thus cone anisotropy is not only a qualitative signature but a quantitatively robust observable probe of defect strength.

\section{What is Not Yet Closed}

The following are \emph{not} yet unconditionally closed:

\item Proton decay prediction.
\item Neutrino mass prediction.
\item Coupling-constant unification prediction.
\item Full matter embedding for realistic baryon/lepton sector phenomenology.
\end{enumerate}

Current honest status:
TECT presently predicts the defect-fermion infrared Branch B Dirac phase much more strongly than it predicts standard GUT observables.

\section{GUT-Style Prediction Assessment}

For the standard GUT-type observables:

\paragraph{Proton decay.}
Not yet predictable at the present stage.
Requires explicit baryon-number-violating operators and their suppression scale.

\paragraph{Neutrino mass.}
Closest possible future target, but not yet predicted.
Requires explicit tiny mass-generation mechanism and neutrino-sector identification.

\paragraph{Coupling unification.}
Not yet predictable.
Requires full gauge normalization, matter content, beta functions, and threshold structure.

Thus at the current stage none of these three is yet quantitatively derived.

\section{Strongest Honest Current Conclusion}

The strongest justified statement at the current stage is:

\text{Within the existing local isotropic TECT algebra, the defect infrared theory is on an exact Branch B anisotropic Dirac phase.}
}

}
\end{equation}

\section{Exact Next Frontier}

The next real frontier is no longer branch selection.
It is quantitative microscopic prediction of the anisotropy ratio:
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}.
\]

More concretely, the next tasks are:

\item only after that, revisit possible links to realistic neutrino-mass generation or later gauge phenomenology.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Current TECT status:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The reduced defect fermion theory is on an exact Branch B phase inside the existing local isotropic TECT algebra.
\item The exact reduced PDE is an anisotropic Dirac equation with coefficients \(v_\parallel,v_E,v_O\).
\item In the natural small-defect regime, the phase is generically stable and strongly transverse-dominated.
\item The key infrared observable is the anisotropy ratio
\[
\rho=\frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp},
\qquad
v_\perp=\sqrt{v_E^2+v_O^2},
\qquad
A=\frac{1}{|\rho|}.
\]
\item In the minimal unified-coupling truncation,

|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=

\]
\item Standard GUT observables such as proton decay, neutrino mass, and coupling unification are not yet quantitatively predicted.
\end{enumerate}

Next frontier:
compute \(\lambda_c^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_E^{(1)},\widetilde\Lambda_O^{(1)}\) in richer full microscopic truncations and sharpen the prediction for \(\rho\).
